Al-Dawani's Objection on the Simple
Al-Dawani objects to the necessity of the simple as a principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Dawani's objection that the termination of composite parts in a simple is not self-evident and that multiplicity requires only a numerical one, not a real one.

Core Idea
The simple as a principle of composition is not self-evident; multiplicity can be accounted for by numerical unity.

Al-Dawani challenges the foundational premise that every composite must ultimately depend on a simple. He argues that while a composite must have parts, it is not obvious that these parts must end at a non-composite. Similarly, multiplicity requires a numerical one, but not necessarily a real one; consider the species 'man' containing individuals that themselves contain other parts. This objection forces us to examine what kind of unity is truly necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read al-Dawani's objection in Arabic and translation. Notice his two-fold challenge: against the simple as a necessary principle, and against the necessity of a real one in multiplicity.
Pay attention to his example of the multiplicity of 'man'.
2
Identify the two arguments
Al-Dawani presents two distinct points: first, that composite parts need not terminate in a simple; second, that multiplicity only requires a numerical one, not a real one. Distinguish these in the passage.
3
Analyze the example of 'man'
Consider how the example illustrates the second point: the one 'man' contains parts that are not man, and those parts may contain further units ad infinitum.
This shows that numerical unity (the one man) is sufficient, without needing a real simple.
4
Consider the implications
If al-Dawani's objection is valid, it challenges the infinite regress argument for a simple divine essence. However, note that a response follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأن البسيط إلخ ) قال المحقق الدواني : لمانع أن يمنع كون البسيط الحقيقي مبدأ للمركب مطلقا إلى أن يقوم عليه البرهان فإن القدر الضروري هو أن المركب لا بد له من أجزاء يتقوم هو بها، وأما انتهاؤها إلى ما ليس بمركب فليس بينا بنفسه، وكذا الكثرة لا بد فيها من الواحد العددي لا من الواحد الحقيقي لجواز اشتماله على آحاد أخر، وهكذا مثلا الكثرة من أفراد الإنسان لا بد فيها من الإنسان الواحد، ثم الإنسان الواحد مشتمل على آحاد أخر لا يكون إنساناً، ويجوز أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء أيضاً مشتملاً على آحاد لا يكون من نوع تلك الآحاد ، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية انتهى، وفيه أن تلك التركيبات ومراتب الكثرة، إذا أخذت بحيث لا يشذ منها واحد لا بد فيها من بسيط وواحد ، ولا يكون ذلك البسيط والواحد حقيقياً، وإلا لم يكن ما فرضناه جميعا، نعم يرد عليه أن الانتهاء إلى البسيط والواحد واجب فيما إذا كان جميع تلك الأجزاء ما منه التركيب، أما إذا كانت انتزاعية فلا بل الواجب حينئذ وجود مبدأ الانتزاع.
His saying: ('Because the simple, etc.') al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said: There is an objector who may prevent the real simple from being a principle of the composite absolutely until a proof is established for it. For the necessary extent is that the composite must have parts by which it is constituted, but their termination in what is not a composite is not self-evident. Likewise, multiplicity must have a numerical one, not a real one, because it may contain other units. For example, the multiplicity of individuals of man must have the one man; then that one man contains other units that are not man; and it is possible that each of those parts also contains units not of the type of those units, and so on ad infinitum. [End of his words.] And there is in it that those compositions and orders of multiplicity, if taken in such a way that not one of them escapes, must have a simple and a one, but that simple and one need not be real; otherwise what we assumed would not be all. Indeed, it is objected to him that the termination in the simple and the one is necessary when all those parts are those from which the composition is made, whereas if they are abstractive, then it is not necessary; rather, what is necessary then is the existence of a source of abstraction.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Dawani against the necessity of ending at a simple (بسيط) in the infinite regress argument. Al-Dawani argues that it is not self-evident that the simple is the principle of all composites; rather, it is necessary only that a composite has parts, but those parts need not end at a non-composite. He gives the example of the species 'human' containing individuals, each of which contains other parts not human. The response clarifies that if we consider all compositions and orders of multiplicity without exception, there must be a simple and one, but this simple and one need not be real; however, if the parts are 'compositional' (ما منه التركيب), the ending at a simple is necessary, whereas if they are 'abstractive' (انتزاعية), then only a principle of abstraction is needed.

Key Terms
البسيط
الواحد العددي
المركب
المبدإ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every series of parts must end at a simple non-composite.
Correction: Al-Dawani argues this is not self-evident; only that a composite has parts, but those parts may be composite themselves indefinitely.

Checkpoint
What is al-Dawani's main objection?
A) That the simple is not a principle of composition
B) That the infinite regress is not impossible
C) That the differentiae cannot be existent
D) That existence is not a part

Key Takeaway
Al-Dawani challenges the necessity of a real simple, arguing that termination in a simple is not self-evident and numerical unity suffices for multiplicity.

?
Al-Dawani uses the example of 'man' to show that a numerical one can contain parts of a different nature. Where in your own reasoning about God have you confused a conceptual unity with a real, simple unity? How would you correct that?

The Response: Compositional vs. Abstractive Parts
The commentator responds by distinguishing compositional and abstractive parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to al-Dawani, which distinguishes between compositional parts (ما منه التركيب) and abstractive parts (انتزاعية), showing that termination in a simple is necessary only for the former.

Core Idea
Not all parts are alike: compositional parts require a simple; abstractive parts only need a source of abstraction.

The commentator responds by clarifying the scope of the argument. He first notes that if we consider all compositions and orders of multiplicity without exception, there must be a simple and one, but not necessarily a real one. However, the crucial distinction is between parts that are truly constitutive (ما منه التركيب) and parts that are merely abstracted from something (انتزاعية). For the former, ending at a simple is necessary; for the latter, only a principle of abstraction is needed. This saves the argument for the divine simple while addressing al-Dawani's objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the response carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the response. Notice the key terms: 'تلك التركيبات ومراتب الكثرة' and the distinction introduced.
2
Understand the two conditions
First, when all compositions are taken without omission, there must be a simple and one, but not necessarily real. Second, the necessity of termination in a simple depends on whether the parts are compositional (ما منه التركيب) or abstractive (انتزاعية).
The phrase 'إذا أخذت بحيث لا يشذ منها واحد' means taking all compositions into account.
3
Distinguish the two types of parts
Compositional parts are those from which something is actually made (real constitute parts). Abstractive parts are conceptual abstractions from something (like attributes from essence).
The example of 'man' likely involves abstractive parts.
4
Reflect on the resolution
This distinction resolves al-Dawani's objection: his example of 'man' involves abstractive parts, so termination in a simple is not required there. But for true compositional parts (e.g., of a material object), termination in a simple is necessary.
This preserves the argument for a divine simple, as divine attributes are abstractive, not compositional.
5
Consider theological significance
The response shows that the argument for a simple God does not depend on the kind of multiplicity al-Dawani raised, because the relationship between attributes and essence is abstractive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[End of his words.] And there is in it that those compositions and orders of multiplicity, if taken in such a way that not one of them escapes, must have a simple and a one, but that simple and one need not be real; otherwise what we assumed would not be all. Indeed, it is objected to him that the termination in the simple and the one is necessary when all those parts are those from which the composition is made, whereas if they are abstractive, then it is not necessary; rather, what is necessary then is the existence of a source of abstraction.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Dawani against the necessity of ending at a simple (بسيط) in the infinite regress argument. Existence Beyond Substance and Accident: Logical Analysis of Addition
Commentator refutes the second proof and presents the author's logical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is not a division of the existent and how the author's logical analysis refines the claim that existence is additional to essence.

Core Idea
Existence is not a part of the existent; it is the condition of being existent.

The commentator addresses a second proof that attempts to force existence into being either substance or accident. He rejects this by clarifying that substance and accident are divisions of the existent, but existence itself is not a division—rather, it is the condition for being existent. The author then deepens the analysis by examining the universal affirmative that all existence is additional to essence, showing where different proofs lead.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two main parts: the refutation of the second proof and the author's logical investigation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the refutation argument
The commentator rejects the claim that existence must be substance or accident. Find the key reason: existence is not a division of the existent. Understand why that matters.
3
Follow the author's logical investigation
The author discusses the universal affirmative proposition that existence is additional to essence. He notes that some proofs prove the full claim, others only common existence. Then he considers the particular negative and the possibility that existence is included only in some essences.
Notice the distinction between universal affirmative and particular negative.
4
Ponder the 'slippery point'
The author explains why he did not prevent the first two entailments but prevented the third. This is a nuanced logical move. Reflect on why preventing the first two would be obstinate denial.
The commentary calls this a slippery point where many have stumbled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزئي، فجاز أن يكون الوجود داخلاً في بعض الماهيات دون بعض فلا تسلسل ويجاب عن الدليل الثاني بأن يقال قوله: الموجود إما جوهر أو عرض قلنا لا جوهر ولا عرض فإنهما أي الجوهر والعرض من أقسام الموجود والوجود ليس من أقسام الموجود لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء مندرجاً تحت المتصف بذلك الشيء. قال المصنف: والتحقيق قال بالزيادة موجبة كلية أي كل وجود مشتركاً كان أو خاصاً زائد على الماهية، إلا أن بعض أدلته يدل على تمام المدعى كالدليلين الأولين وبعضها يدل على زيادة الوجود المشترك كالدليل الثالث والرابع ونقيض الموجبة الكلية السالبة الجزئية، أي ليس كل وجود زائداً ففيما نحن فيه يكون المدعى زيادة الوجود المطلق في جميع الماهيات الممكنة، فجاز أن يكون صدق نقيضه أعني سلب زيادته في جميع الماهيات بأن يكون داخلاً في البعض دون البعض، فلا نسلم أن المفروض أنه جنس للموجودات بل المفروض أنه جنس لبعض الماهيات لأنه اللازم من عدم زيادته في الجميع فإن قيل: إذا كان المدعى زيادته في جميع الماهيات كان معنى قوله لو لم يكن زائداً في الجميع لكان نفسها أو جزءاً منها نفس بعضها أو جزء بعضها، فحينئذ يمكن منع الملازمة الأولى أعني لزوم اتحاد الماهيات لجواز أن يكون نفس ماهية واحدة، فلا يلزم اتحاد ماهيتين فضلا اتحاد الماهيات وكذا على تقدير الجزئية يمكن منع قوله لكان أعم عن الذاتيات، لجواز أن يكون ذاتياً مختصاً بماهية واحدة فلم لم يمنع هاتين الملازمتين ومنع الملازمة الأخيرة أعني قوله فكان جنساً للفصول قلت: لما كان القول بأن الوجود المشترك نفس بعض الماهيات أو ذاتي مختص ببعض الماهيات مكابرة أعرض عن منع تينك الملازمتين بخلاف الملازمة الثالثة، تأمل فإنه من المداحض التي زل فيها الأقدام. قوله: ليس من أقسام الموجود بل هو معدوم ولا يلزم من اعتبار المعروض في شيء اعتبار العارض، وإلا لامتنع التركيب مطلقا لأن كل جزء من المركب متصف بنقيضه، فلا يلزم من جزئيته للجوهر والعرض أن لا يكونا موجودين فلا يرد ما قيل إنه إذا لم يكن من أقسام الموجود لم يكن جزءاً للجوهر والعرض لأن جزء الموجود موجود فثبت المطلوب وهو عدم الجزئية، وكذا ما قيل: إذا لم يكن جوهراً ولا عرضاً لم يكن جزءاً منهما لأن جزء الجوهر جوهر وجزء العرض عرض.
Partial, so it is possible that existence is included in some essences without others, so there is no infinite regress. And it is answered regarding the second proof by saying: His statement: 'The existent is either substance or accident.' We say: No, not substance nor accident, for they (substance and accident) are divisions of the existent, and existence is not a division of the existent, because it is impossible for a thing to be subsumed under that which is qualified by that same thing. The author said: And the investigation: He said regarding the addition a universal affirmative, i.e., every existence, whether common or specific, is additional to the essence, except that some of his proofs indicate the complete claim like the first two proofs, and some indicate the addition of common existence like the third and fourth proofs. The contradictory of the universal affirmative is the particular negative, i.e., not every existence is additional. So in our case, the claim is the addition of absolute existence in all possible essences. So it is possible that the truth of its contradictory, i.e., the negation of its addition in all essences by being included in some without others, so we do not accept that the supposed is that it is a genus of existents, but rather the supposed is that it is a genus of some essences because that is necessary from its not being additional in all. For if it is said: When the claim is its addition in all essences, the meaning of his statement 'if it were not additional in all, it would be identical to them or a part of them' would be identical to some of them or a part of some of them. Then the first entailment, i.e., the necessity of the unification of essences, can be prevented because it might be identical to a single essence, so the unification of two essences does not follow, let alone the unification of all essences. Similarly, on the supposition of partiality, his statement 'it would be more general than the essential properties' can be prevented, because it might be an essential property specific to a single essence. So why did he not prevent these two entailments and prevent the last entailment, i.e., his statement 'so it would be a genus of the differentiae'? I said: Because the statement that common existence is identical to some essences or an essential property specific to some essences is an obstinate denial, he refrained from preventing those two entailments, unlike the third entailment. Ponder, for it is one of the slippery points. His statement: 'It is not a division of the existent, but rather it is non-existent, and it is not necessary from considering the subject in something to consider the accident, otherwise composition would be absolutely impossible, because every part of a composite is qualified by its opposite, so it is not necessary from its being a part of substance and accident that they would not be existent. So the objection is not valid that said: If it is not a division of the existent, then it is not a part of substance and accident, because a part of an existent is existent. Thus the desired conclusion—namely, the denial of partiality—is established. Similarly, the objection that said: If it is not substance nor accident, then it is not a part of them, because a part of substance is substance and a part of accident is accident.'
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage combines the commentator's response to the second proof (that existence must be substance or accident) and the author's (al-musannif's) logical investigation (al-tahqiq) into the claim of addition. The first part refutes the proof by asserting that existence is not a division of the existent, because it is impossible for something to be subsumed under that which qualifies it. The second part (the author's tahqiq) discusses the universal affirmative proposition that all existence is additional, noting that only some proofs prove the full claim while others prove only common existence. The author then considers the contradictory (particular negative) and argues that the claim of addition to all essences is not necessary; it could be that existence is included only in some. The commentary also addresses potential objections about the impossibility of composition if existence were not a division, clarifying that part-whole relations do not require existence to be a division of the existent. Overall, this lesson establishes the logical groundwork for the debate on whether existence is identical to essence or additional.

Misconception: One might think that if existence is not a substance or accident, it must be non-existent.
Correction: The passage clarifies that existence is not a division of the existent, but that does not mean it is non-existent; it is the condition for being existent.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the claim that existence must be either substance or accident?
A) Because existence is a substance.

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that existence is not a division of the existent, and the author clarifies that the claim of universal addition is not fully supported by all proofs, allowing for the possibility that existence is not additional in every essence.

?
The author refrains from preventing certain entailments, labeling them as 'obstinate denial'. Are there beliefs you hold that you have never subjected to logical scrutiny, perhaps because they seem obvious? Can you identify one such belief and test it against the sources?
?
Existence is not a division of the existent—it underlies everything you are. Do you live as if your existence is a gift to be analyzed and grateful for, or do you take it for granted? What one change can you make today to be more mindful of the reality of your own existence?

Arguments Show Conceptual Distinction Only
Commentator explains that the arguments for addition show only conceptual distinction, not real distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the common arguments for existence being additional to essence only prove a conceptual distinction, not a real distinction in the external world.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinction does not equal real distinction.

This passage clarifies a common misunderstanding about the classical proofs for the addition of existence. The commentator shows that while the proofs demonstrate a conceptual difference, they do not necessarily prove that existence and essence are distinct realities. The debate hinges on real distinction, not just conceptual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by carefully reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Try to grasp the overall argument before diving into analysis.
Pay attention to the key terms: تغاير المفهومين and تغاير الذاتين.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that the arguments only indicate a distinction of concepts, not a distinction of essences. Locate this statement in the text.
The phrase 'إنما تفيد تغاير المفهومين دون تغاير الذاتين' is central.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters. The debate is about whether existence is additional to essence in reality, not just in thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء إلخ أي لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء مندرجاً تحت المتصف بذلك الشيء بعينه من غير اعتبار تغاير بينهما اتصافاً حقيقياً لأنه يستلزم اتصاف الشيء بنفسه وهو محال لعدم التغاير بين الشيء ونفسه فلا يرد أن العدم مندرج تحت المعدوم، لأن اتصاف المعدوم بالعدم ليس حقيقياً ولا أن مفهوم العلم بعد تعلق العلم به، ومفهوم الكلي وأمثالهما مندرج تحت المعلوم والكلي، لأن ذلك بعد اعتبار التغاير بينهما، وفيما نحن فيه ليس كذلك لأن الوجود المطلق لو كان موجوداً لا يكون وصفه حصة من الوجود المطلق عارضة له بل الخصوصية إنما تحصل له بعد العروض. قوله: والتحقيق أن هذه الوجوه إلخ أما غير الوجه الأول فظاهر وأما الوجه الأول فقيل: لأنه مثل أن يقال الأب ليس عين زيد لأن الأب يمتنع أن يكون لا أباً، وزيد قد يكون لا أباً، ولا يخفى أنه يفيد المغايرة بحسب المفهوم، والحق أن خلاصة الوجه الأول هو أن ذات الماهية تقبل العدم، فلو كان الوجود نفسها أو جزءها لما كان كذلك فيفيد التغاير بين الذاتين فتأمل. أن هذه الوجوه التي استدل بها على كون الوجود زائداً على ماهية الممكن إنما تفيد تغاير المفهومين أي مفهوم الوجود ومفهوم السواد مثلا دون تغاير الذاتين أي ذات الوجود وذات السواد مثلا والنزاع إنما وقع فيه أي في تغاير الذاتين لا في تغاير المفهومين فإن عاقلا لا يقول: مفهوم السواد هو بعينه مفهوم الوجود بل يقول العاقل إن ما صدق عليه السواد من الأمور الخارجية هو بعينه ما صدق عليه الوجود وليس لهما أي للوجود والسواد هويتان متمايزتان في الخارج تقوم إحداهما بالأخرى كالسواد القائم بالجسم فإن للسواد هوية ممتازة عن هوية الجسم بحسب الخارج، وقد قامت.
His statement: 'because it is impossible for a thing etc.' i.e., because it is impossible for a thing to be subsumed under that which is qualified by that very same thing without considering a distinction between them, in a real attribution, because it entails the attribution of a thing to itself, which is impossible due to the lack of distinction between a thing and itself. So it is not objected that non-existence is subsumed under the non-existent, because the attribution of non-existence to the non-existent is not real, nor that the concept of knowledge after its attachment to the known, and the concept of the universal and the like are subsumed under the known and the universal, because that is after considering the distinction between them. In our case, it is not so, because absolute existence, if it were existent, its description would not be a portion of absolute existence that is accidental to it, but rather the particularity is only obtained for it after the attribution. His statement: 'And the investigation that these arguments etc.' As for other than the first argument, it is apparent. As for the first argument, it is said: It is like saying 'the father is not identical to Zayd' because the father cannot be without a father, while Zayd can be without a father, and it is clear that it indicates difference in terms of concept. The truth is that the essence of the first argument is that the essence of the mahiyya (essence) admits non-existence, so if existence were identical to it or a part of it, it would not be so. Thus it indicates distinction between the two essences. So ponder. That these arguments, which were used to prove that existence is additional to the essence of the possible, only indicate the distinction of the two concepts—i.e., the concept of existence and the concept of blackness, for example—not the distinction of the two essences (i.e., the essence of existence and the essence of blackness, for example). The debate is only about that, i.e., the distinction of the two essences, not the distinction of the two concepts. For no rational person says that the concept of blackness is identical to the concept of existence. Rather, the rational person says that what blackness applies to among external things is identical to what existence applies to, and they (existence and blackness) do not have two distinct identities in the external, one subsisting through the other, like blackness subsisting in the body, for blackness has an identity distinct from the identity of the body in terms of the external, and it subsists.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the earlier statement about the impossibility of subsumption and then a detailed analysis of the value of the arguments for the addition of existence. The commentator clarifies an objection about non-existence being subsumed under the non-existent, explaining that such attribution is not real. He then turns to the claim that the arguments (al-wujuh) for addition only prove a conceptual distinction between existence and essence, not a real distinction in the external world. He notes that the first argument, often misunderstood, actually reduces to the point that the essence admits non-existence, which would not be the case if existence were identical or a part; this indicates a real distinction. However, he emphasizes that the debate is about real distinction, and the first three arguments only show conceptual difference. The fourth argument, by contrast, does show real distinction. The passage thus refines the understanding of the proofs, guarding against the mistake of thinking that conceptual distinction suffices for the claim of addition.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the debate is about real distinction (tighayur al-dhatayn), not merely conceptual distinction (tighayur al-mafhumayn). The arguments only show the latter.

The arguments for the addition of existence demonstrate that the concepts of existence and essence are distinct, but they do not prove that existence and essence are separate entities in the external world.

Why Only the Fourth Proof Shows Real Distinction
Commentator evaluates arguments for addition vs identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that only the fourth proof demonstrates a real distinction in essence between existence and essence, while the first three only show conceptual distinction.

Core Idea
Only one of the four arguments for addition truly proves distinction in essence.

The commentator carefully examines the arguments for the addition doctrine, revealing that most only show conceptual difference. The key is to identify which argument actually requires real distinction in essence. This lesson walks through the reasoning, showing why the fourth proof stands apart.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentator explicitly states his aim: to give preponderance to the identity doctrine.
2
Identify the three arguments that fail
The passage discusses three arguments that only indicate conceptual distinction. Find each one and note the reason given for why it does not prove real distinction.
The first argument is about essence and non-existence; the second about doubt; the third about predication.
3
Understand why the fourth argument succeeds
The commentator says the fourth argument requires distinction in essence by negating identity and partiality. Grasp why this is different and why it leads to real distinction.
The fourth argument is the one that the author (al-Shaykh) relies on.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Ask yourself: if only one argument proves real distinction, does that vindicate the identity doctrine? The commentator seems to think so.
This connects to the broader debate between addition and identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: والتحقيق أي بيان الحق من قولي الزيادة والعينية بعد الإحاطة بدلائل الطرفين والمقصود منه ترجيح مذهب العينية، وخلاصته أن التغاير من حيث المفهوم لا يقبل النزاع فلا يمكن حمل الاختلاف عليه فالاختلاف والنزاع إنما هو في التغاير من حيث الذات، والحق في ذلك مذهب الشيخ لدليل لاح له. قوله: إن هذه الوجوه إلخ أي ما سوى الوجه الرابع بقرينة أنه يدل على زيادة الوجود المطلق والشيخ لا يقول به. قوله: إنما تفيد تغاير المفهومين أما الأول فلان مبناه على اختلاف الماهية الموجودة والماهية من حيث هي في قبول العدم وعدمه، وذلك إنما يدل على اختلاف الاعتبارين لا على اختلاف الذاتين ألا يرى أن الإنسان من حيث هو يقبل عدم الكتابة والمأخوذ مع الكتابة لا يقبله مع اتحادهما في الذات، وأما الثاني فلانه يجوز الشك في ثبوت شيء لشيء إذا كانا متغايرين في المفهوم مع اتحادهما ذاتا كما في هذا زيد أي مسمى بزيد، وأما الثالث فلان إفادة الحمل إنما تغاير الطرفين مفهوماً لا ذاتاً، بل يقتضي الاتحاد فيه بخلاف الوجه الرابع، فإنه يقتضي التغاير في الذات، فإن نفي النفسية والجزئية يستلزم التغاير في الذات فمن قال: دلالة الوجه الرابع على تغاير المفهوم ظاهرة فقد خفي عليه الظاهر.
His statement: 'And the investigation' i.e., the clarification of the truth regarding the two opinions of addition and identity after encompassing the proofs of the two parties, and its purpose is to give preponderance to the doctrine of identity. Its summary is that distinction in terms of concept is not subject to dispute, so disagreement cannot be attributed to it; rather, disagreement and debate are only about distinction in terms of essence. The truth in that is the doctrine of al-Shaykh due to a proof that became clear to him. His statement: 'These arguments etc.' i.e., other than the fourth argument, by indication that it indicates the addition of absolute existence, and al-Shaykh does not hold that. His statement: 'They only indicate the distinction of the two concepts' — as for the first, it is because its basis is the difference between the existing essence and the essence in itself in terms of accepting non-existence and not accepting it, and that only indicates the difference of the two considerations, not the difference of the two essences. Do you not see that man in himself accepts the non-existence of writing, while man taken with writing does not accept it, despite the unity of the two in essence. As for the second, it is because doubt is permissible about the existence of something for something when the two are distinct in concept while united in essence, as in 'this Zayd,' i.e., named Zayd. As for the third, it is because predication only requires the distinction of the two terms in concept, not in essence; rather, it requires unity in essence. This is contrary to the fourth argument, for it requires distinction in essence, because the negation of identity and partiality entails distinction in essence. So whoever says that the indication of the fourth argument for conceptual distinction is apparent, the apparent has been hidden from him.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the author's investigation, explicitly stating that the purpose of the investigation is to determine the truth between the doctrines of addition (ziyada) and identity (ayniyya) of existence and essence, and that the author (al-Shaykh al-Asha'iri?) sides with the identity doctrine based on a certain proof. The commentator then specifies that the arguments except the fourth only show conceptual distinction, and he explains why each of the first three arguments fails to prove real distinction: the first relies on a difference in considerations, not essences; the second allows for doubt about something's existence for something despite conceptual difference when essences are united; the third shows that predication implies conceptual distinction but essential unity. Only the fourth argument, which negates identity and partiality, requires real distinction in essence. The passage thus carefully delineates the value of each proof and defends the author's position that the fourth argument is the key one for proving real addition.

Why the First Three Arguments Only Show Conceptual Difference
Commentator refutes three arguments for real distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first three arguments for the addition of existence at most prove a conceptual distinction between essence and existence, not a real one.

Core Idea
Conceptual difference does not imply real difference in kalam.

In this passage, the commentator examines three common arguments for the addition of existence to essence. He shows that each argument only establishes a difference in how we conceive of the two, not a real separation. This is a crucial methodological point in kalam: not every logical distinction corresponds to an ontological one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: the commentator addresses three arguments in sequence, then contrasts with a fourth.
2
Analyze each argument's flaw
For each of the first three arguments, identify why the commentator says it only shows conceptual difference. Pay attention to examples like man and writing, or 'this is Zayd'.
3
Contrast with the fourth argument
The commentator mentions that the fourth argument does prove real difference because it negates selfness and partiality. Understand why this criterion is stronger.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this principle applies in kalam: not every logical distinction corresponds to a real separation. This guards against multiplying entities unnecessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إن هذه الوجوه إلخ) أي ما سوى الوجه الرابع بقرينة أنه يدل على زيادة الوجود المطلق والشيخ لا يقول به . قوله : ( إنما تفيد تغاير المفهومين) أما الأول فلان مبناه على اختلاف الماهية الموجودة والماهية من حيث هي في قبول العدم وعدمه، وذلك إنما يدل على اختلاف الاعتبارين لا على اختلاف الذاتين ألا يرى أن الإنسان من حيث هو يقبل عدم الكتابة والمأخوذ مع الكتابة لا يقبله مع اتحادهما في الذات، وأما الثاني فلانه يجوز الشك في ثبوت شيء لشيء إذا كانا متغايرين في المفهوم مع اتحادهما ذاتا كما في هذا زيد أي مسمى بزيد، وأما الثالث فلان إفادة الحمل إنما تغاير الطرفين مفهوماً لا ذاتاً، بل يقتضي الاتحاد فيه بخلاف الوجه الرابع، فإنه يقتضي التغاير في الذات، فإن نفي النفسية والجزئية يستلزم التغاير في الذات فمن قال : دلالة الوجه الرابع على تغاير المفهوم ظاهرة فقد خفي عليه الظاهر. يستدعي قوله : (لا يقول إلخ) فإنه يحكم بأن السواد موجود وليس بموجود وكلاهما ممتنعان عند الاتحاد في المفهوم. قوله : ( من الأمور الخارجية قيد بذلك لأن ما صدق عليه السواد من الأمور الذهنية مغاير لما صدق عليه الوجود فإن الأول هوية خارجية والثاني أمر اعتباري . قوله : ( هويتان) أي ماهيتان شخصيتان . قوله : ( في الخارج بل متمايزان في الذهن .
His statement: (That these aspects etc.) i.e., other than the fourth aspect, by indication that it proves the addition of absolute existence and the Sheikh does not hold it. His statement: (Only indicate the difference of the two concepts) As for the first, its basis is the difference between the existing essence and the essence in itself regarding the acceptance of non-existence and its non-acceptance, and that only indicates a difference of two considerations, not a difference of the two essences. Do you not see that man in himself accepts the absence of writing, but man taken with writing does not accept it, despite their unity in essence. As for the second, doubt is permissible about the subsistence of a thing for a thing when they are conceptually different but essentially united, as in 'this is Zayd' i.e., named Zayd. As for the third, predication only requires the conceptual difference of the two terms, not essential difference, but rather demands unity in essence, unlike the fourth aspect, for it demands essential difference, because the negation of selfness and partiality necessitates essential difference. So whoever says that the indication of the fourth aspect on conceptual difference is apparent, the apparent is hidden from him. His statement: (He does not say etc.) For he judges that blackness is existent and not existent, both of which are impossible when concepts are united. His statement: (From external things) qualified by that because what blackness applies to from mental things is different from what existence applies to, for the first is an external identity and the second is a consideration. His statement: (Two identities) i.e., two personal essences. His statement: (In the external world, rather they are distinct in the mind.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on why the first three arguments for the addition of existence only prove conceptual difference. The commentator explains each: The first argument is based on the difference between an existing essence and the essence in itself regarding the acceptance of non-existence; this only shows a difference in how we consider the essence, not a real distinction. The second argument is that doubt is possible when two things are conceptually distinct but essentially united, like 'Zayd' and 'named Zayd'. The third argument involves predication, which only requires conceptual difference of subject and predicate, not real difference. In contrast, the fourth argument does prove real difference because negating selfness and partiality necessitates that existence is not identical to essence nor a part of it. The commentator then clarifies a qualification: 'from external things' is added because what blackness applies to mentally is different from what existence applies to (mental vs. external). Finally, 'two identities' means two personal essences, distinct only in the mind, not externally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any conceptual difference between essence and existence proves they are really distinct.
Correction: The first three arguments only show a difference in our consideration (i'tibar), not in reality.

Key Takeaway
The first three arguments for the addition of existence only establish a conceptual distinction, not a real one; only the fourth argument proves real difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you treat it as something separate from your essence — as if your being is a possession rather than a continuous act of Allah's will? How would your life change if you truly felt that at every moment your existence is identical to your essence, completely dependent on Him? Can you find one concrete thing today to do that acknowledges that dependence?
?
The commentator shows that predication — saying 'the essence is existent' — requires essential unity. Now think about your own statements like 'I am a Muslim' or 'I am sincere'. Do your words and actions match? Or are they conceptually different, like two separate things? Without deception, what is one area where your claim of faith does not yet have essential unity with your practice, and what step will you take to unite them?
?
This lesson warns against mistaking conceptual difference for real separation. In your relationship with Allah, are you content with knowing about His attributes conceptually, or do you strive for a real, living connection? If reading this passage has shown you a gap between your concept of faith and its reality in your heart, what is the first sacrifice you can make today to close that gap?

The Proof That Existence Is Identical to Quiddity
States and proves the identity of existence and quiddity in extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that existence and quiddity are identical in extension and why distinct identities lead to absurdity.

Core Idea
Existence and quiddity are one in the external world—separating them leads to a regress.

This passage from al-Iji's Mawqif presents the central thesis that existence and quiddity are not two distinct entities in the external world. Al-Ash'ari's proof shows that if they were distinct, quiddity would have existence before existence is added—an impossibility. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the structure: thesis, proof by contradiction, conclusion.
The passage contains a commentary insertion; focus on the core argument.
2
Identify the thesis
The passage begins with the thesis: existence is the same as quiddity or a part of it. The preferred view is the first: identity in extension.
3
Follow the reductio proof
The proof assumes the opposite (distinct identities) and derives an absurdity: quiddity would have existence before existence. Understand the logical flow.
The proof uses an analogy with body and blackness.
4
Connect to al-Ash'ari
The passage notes that this is the meaning of al-Ash'ari's statement. Reflect on how this argument grounds the Ash'ari position.
5
Clarify the added phrase
The commentary explains why the phrase 'and existence would have another identity' is added. Note that the absurdity follows even without that phrase.
This addresses a potential misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها الأولى بالثانية (و) ما ذكره من أن ما صدق عليه أحدهما هو عين ما صدق عليه الآخر، وأنه ليس لهما هويتان متمايزتان ( هو الحق) المطابق للواقع ( وإلا لكان للماهية هوية ممتازة في الخارج ( مع قطع النظر عن الوجود ) وكان للوجود أيضاً هوية أخرى حتى يمكن قيامها بهوية السواد في الخارج كما أن للجسم هوية خارجية مع قطع النظر عن السواد وللسواد هوية أخرى حتى أمكن قيام السواد بالجسم في الخارج ( فكان لها ) أي للماهية ( قبل ) انضمام ( الوجود ) إليها ( وجود ) ، فيلزم ما مر من المحذورات ( وهو معنى كلام الشيخ ( أبي الحسن الأشعري (وفحوى دليله) لأنه يدل على امتناع كون الوجود متمايز الهوية عن هويات الماهيات الموجودة قوله : ( وكان للوجود إلخ زاده على المتن لأنه اللازم من قوله وإلا أي أن لا يكون النفي المذكور أي ليس لهما هويتان متمایزتان بل كان لهما هويتان متمايزتان في الخارج، لا لأن ترتب قوله : فكان لها إلخ، موقوف عليه فإنه لازم من مجرد أن يكون للماهية هوية ممتازة الخارج.
The First Observatory - The Third Objective: That existence is the same as quiddity or a part of it. The first is more appropriate. (And) what he mentioned, that what one of them applies to is exactly what the other applies to, and that they do not have two distinct identities, (is the truth) conforming to reality. (Otherwise quiddity would have a distinct identity in the external world) (with disregard of existence) and existence would also have another identity such that it could subsist in the identity of blackness in the external world, just as a body has an external identity with disregard of blackness and blackness has another identity such that blackness could subsist in the body in the external world. (Then quiddity would have, before the conjunction of existence to it, existence), thus follows the aforementioned absurdities. (And this is the meaning of the speech of the Shaykh (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) and the import of his proof) because it indicates the impossibility of existence having an identity distinct from the identities of existing quiddities. His statement: (And existence would have, etc.) He added it to the text because it is entailed by his statement 'otherwise', meaning that the aforementioned negation, that they do not have two distinct identities, is not the case; rather they have two distinct identities in the external world, not because the ordering of his statement 'then it would have, etc.' depends on it, for it is entailed merely by quiddity having a distinct identity in the external world.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the main thesis and its proof. It begins by stating that the correct view is that existence and quiddity are identical in extension: whatever existence applies to, quiddity applies to, and they do not have two separate identities. The proof is by reductio: if they had distinct identities in the external world, then quiddity would have an identity of its own apart from existence, and existence would have another identity; then quiddity would exist before existence is added to it, which leads to absurdities mentioned earlier. This is the meaning of al-Ash'ari's statement and the import of his proof: it shows that existence cannot have an identity distinct from the identities of existing things. The commentary then clarifies that the phrase 'and existence would have another identity' was added to the text because it is entailed by the conditional 'otherwise'; it is not that the ordering of the consequence depends on it, for the absurdity follows merely from quiddity having a distinct identity in the external world.

الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof directly proves identity in essence (identity of identity) rather than identity in predication (extension).
Correction: The passage shows a lack of distinction in external existence, which leads to the conclusion that existence and quiddity are identical in what they apply to, not necessarily that they are the same concept.

Checkpoint

A) Quiddity would be prior to existence.

D) Quiddity would be non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Existence and quiddity are not two distinct realities in the external world; if they were, quiddity would have existence prior to existence, which is impossible.

?
The absurdity of quiddity having existence before existence shows the impossibility of separating your reality from your existence. How does this truth confront your tendency to see your flaws as somehow apart from your true self? What will you do to align your actions with your claimed identity as a servant of Allah?

Objection: Lack of Distinction Does Not Imply Identity
Raises and answers the objection that lack of external distinction does not guarantee identity in predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the lack of external distinction between existence and existent does not necessitate their identity in predication, and how the possibility of existence being a mental consideration undermines the identity claim.

Core Idea
External indistinction can exist without identity — one thing may be mental.

Theologians often argue that existence and existent are identical because they are not distinct in the external world like blackness and black. However, this objection points out that lack of external distinction does not guarantee unity in predication (i.e., that they apply to the same thing). The passage then provides a sophisticated answer: existence might be a mental consideration with no external identity, which would explain why the two are not identical in extension.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the objection and the subsequent response.
Pay attention to the flow from objection to answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the objector states that the earlier proof only shows no external distinction, not unity in predication.
The objector's key point: 'لا يستلزم اتحادهما في الصدق'
3
Understand the answer
The answer posits that existence might be a mental consideration (amr i‘tibari) without external identity, so it cannot be identical to the external quiddity.
This counters the assumption that lack of distinction implies identity of identity.
4
Connect to predication theory
Note that unity in predication does not require unity in external identity, as shown by privative attributes like 'blind'.
The passage hints at a broader interpretation of predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in it there is a discussion. Because what he mentioned indicates that existence and the existent are not distinct in the external world, like the distinction between blackness and black; however, this does not entail that the identity of existence in the external world is the same as the identity of the existent. His statement: (And in it there is a discussion) i.e., in his statement 'it is the truth' there is a discussion. Because what he mentioned, from his statement 'otherwise it would have, etc.', indicates the negation of external distinction between them, but it does not indicate their unity in predication, which is the claim. And the interpretation of the Shaykh's speech is that the lack of external distinction entails unity in identity; but it is not so, because it is possible that the lack of distinction is due to existence having no external identity, being a mental consideration (‘itibari) that occurs in the mind. In that case, they are not united in what they apply to: quiddity is an external thing and what existence applies to is mental. By this, what is mistakenly understood from the apparent wording of his clause 'such that what one of them applies to is exactly what the other applies to' is refuted—that unity in predication depends on unity in identity; it is not so, because it will be explained in the discussion of quiddity that the interpretation of predication as unity in external identity is valid only for essential attributes, not for privative attributes like 'Zayd is blind', for blindness has no external identity (otherwise it would be an external existent). The comprehensive interpretation for both is unity in predication, since there is no impossibility in predication of privatives to external existents. This is because his purpose here is that lack of distinction does not entail unity in predication, i.e., that it does not entail unity in identity, and it is not that... based on...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The objector notes that the earlier proof only shows that existence and existent are not distinct in the external world like blackness and black; it does not show that they are identical in predication (i.e., that the same thing is called both by name). The commentator explains that the Shaykh's claim of 'truth' is disputed because the argument only negates external distinction, not that it proves unity in predication (which is the claim). The interpretation of al-Ash'ari's speech would require that lack of external distinction entails identity of identity (huwiyya), but this is not necessary because the lack of distinction could be because existence has no external identity at all—it could be a mental consideration (amr i‘tibari) that supervenes in the mind. In that case, quiddity is external and existence is mental; they are not united in extension. This refutes the misunderstanding that the clause 'such that what applies to one applies to the other' implies that unity in predication depends on unity in identity. The commentator points to the future discussion on quiddity where predication is explained as unity in identity only for essential attributes, not for privatives like 'Zayd is blind' (since blindness has no external identity). The comprehensive interpretation for both cases is unity in predication (ittihad fi al-sidq). Thus the author's intent is that lack of distinction does not entail unity in predication nor unity in identity.

Misconception: Lack of external distinction between two concepts means they are identical in all respects, including their reality in the external world.
Correction: The passage explains that one of them (existence) might be a mental consideration with no external identity, so they are not identical. External indistinguishability can arise from one being purely mental.

Key Takeaway

Existence as a Secondary Intelligible: The Blackness Example
Argues against the identity of existence and quiddity using blackness as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot be identical with quiddity in the external world, because existence is a secondary intelligible without external identity.

Core Idea
Existence is not an external attribute; it is a rational concept predicated on quiddity.

This passage uses the example of blackness (sawad) to argue against the identity of existence and quiddity. It shows that if existence were identical with quiddity, it would have to be predicated as a 'load-bearing' predicate (muwata’ah) like blackness, which would imply that existence is an external attribute—but this is not the case. Instead, existence is a secondary intelligible, a concept that has no external identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main argument
Read the Arabic passage along with the translation. Notice that the author uses blackness as an example to illustrate the relationship between existence and quiddity.
Pay attention to the conditional statement starting with 'kayfa wa law ittahada...'.
2
Understand the concept of secondary intelligibles
The passage states that existence has no external identity because it is among the secondary intelligibles (al-ma‘qulat al-thaniyya). Consider what this means: secondary intelligibles are concepts that exist only in the mind, not in external reality.
This is a key term: المعقولات الثانية.
3
Analyze the consequences of identity
The passage imagines what would happen if existence were identical with quiddity in the external world: it would be predicated as a 'load-bearing' predicate like blackness. Reflect on why this would be problematic for the philosophers' view.
The conclusion is that union in predication requires union in identity.

Like blackness, for example, such that what one of them applies to is exactly what the other applies to, because it is possible that the predication of non-distinction [occurs] by existence having no external identity because it is among the secondary intelligibles. In summary, the identity firmly established in concrete realities is the identity of blackness, and existence is an accident (‘arid) to it. His statement: (And also there would be no, etc.) because the lack of distinction in the external world is known to everyone, for he knows that being described by existence is not like being described by whiteness. So if that entailed union in identity, then the union in identity would also be known after attending to them, and no doubt would remain after that about the existence of existence—since that after knowledge of the existence of blackness is among the most evident theoretical matters. So it cannot be objected that doubt may remain due to lack of knowledge of the union. His statement: (And in summary, the identity, etc.) The fa' is consequential, i.e., when you know the detailed exposition, then the identity, etc.; or it is redundant for mere embellishment. His statement: (until what one of them applies to is exactly what the other applies to) It is said: in making this a corollary of the union of the two identities there is a discussion, because sometimes the extension is united without union of identity, as in the case of privatives like 'Zayd is blind'. And the explicit wording of the author indicates union of extensions, not identities, since he did not state explicitly union of the two identities, but rather negation of their distinctness, and their non-distinctness could be due to the non-existence of one of them. The answer is that the context of the author's speech indicates that he inferred the union of extension from the negation of distinctness of the two identities based on it entailing absurdities; or that he intended by union of extension the union of identity, otherwise his claim of union of extension would be without proof, even though his purpose is to establish this union. So the gist of the discussion: its objection is apparent on the second interpretation; on the first, the negation of distinctness of the two identities does not entail their union so as to entail union of extension. Yes, extension may be united without union of identity, as you know, but the talk here is about the necessity of such union and certainty about it, so let it be contemplated. His statement: (would be) predicated on that essence (as a 'load-bearing' predicate, there is a discussion because union in existence is not...

Commentary
This passage is a multi-faceted commentary that continues the objection and answer, now focusing on the example of blackness (sawad). It argues that if existence were identical with quiddity (like blackness) in the external world, then existence would be a 'load-bearing' predicate (muwata’ah) like blackness, implying that existence would be an external attribute—which is not the case. Instead, existence is a secondary intelligible (ma‘qulat thaniyya) without external identity. The passage then addresses the point that union in predication (ittihad fi al-sidq) does not necessarily require union in identity (ittihad fi al-huwiyya), using the example of 'Zayd is blind' (a privative). The commentator critiques a misconception that the author did not claim that lack of distinction entails union in identity but rather union in predication. He then explains the different possible interpretations of the author's intention: either he inferred union of extension from lack of distinction based on absurdities, or he meant union of identity by 'union of extension'. In the first case, lack of distinction of identities does not entail their union; in the second case, the argument is direct but requires that lack of distinction implies identity. The passage ends with a note that the matter requires further consideration (falyata’ammal).

Key Terms

المواطأة
السواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that existence is an attribute like blackness and therefore can be identical with quiddity.
Correction: This passage shows that existence is a secondary intelligible without external identity, so it cannot be an external attribute identical with quiddity.

Checkpoint

Existence is a secondary intelligible and cannot be identical with quiddity in the external world.

Union in Predication vs. Union in Identity: The Privative Example
Clarifies the distinction between union in predication and union in identity using the example of privatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between union in predication (ittihad fi al-sidq) and union in identity (ittihad fi al-huwiyya), and how privatives like 'Zayd is blind' demonstrate that predication can occur without identity.

Core Idea
Union in predication does not necessitate union in essence; privative attributes are a clear example.

This passage addresses a crucial misconception: some think that if two terms have the same extension (maṣdaq), they must be identical in essence. The author refutes this by using the example of a privative attribute like 'blindness'. When we say 'Zayd is blind', the predicate 'blind' does not have an external identity; it refers to a lack. Yet the predication is valid. This shows that union in predication can occur without union in identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the misconception
The passage begins by stating a misconception: that the author did not claim lack of distinction entails union in identity, but only union in predication. It then uses the example of 'Zayd is blind' to argue that predication can be valid without identity of essence.
The word 'tawahhama' (توهم) indicates a misconception.
2
Understand the privative example
In the case of 'Zayd is blind', blindness is a privative attribute (ma'dumiyyat), meaning it has no positive existence. Yet the statement is true. This shows that union in extension does not require the predicate to have an external identity.
Privative attributes are called العدميات.
3
Analyze the discussion of the author's intention
The commentary then debates whether the original author meant union of extension or union of identity. The key point is that lack of distinction of identities does not necessarily entail their union; it could be due to the non-existence of one of them.
The passage ends with 'falyata'ammal' (so let it be contemplated), indicating the matter is subtle.

And the explicit wording of the author indicates union of extensions, not identities, since he did not state explicitly union of the two identities, but rather negation of their distinctness, and their non-distinctness could be due to the non-existence of one of them. The answer is that the context of the author's speech indicates that he inferred the union of extension from the negation of distinctness of the two identities based on it entailing absurdities; or that he intended by union of extension the union of identity, otherwise his claim of union of extension would be without proof, even though his purpose is to establish this union. So the gist of the discussion: its objection is apparent on the second interpretation; on the first, the negation of distinctness of the two identities does not entail their union so as to entail union of extension. Commentary
This passage is a multi-faceted commentary that continues the objection and answer, now focusing on the example of blackness (sawad). It argues that if existence were identical with quiddity (like blackness) in the external world, then existence would be a 'load-bearing' predicate (muwata’ah) like blackness, implying that existence would be an external attribute—which is not the case. Instead, existence is a secondary intelligible (ma‘qulat thaniyya) without external identity. The passage then addresses the point that union in predication (ittihad fi al-sidq) does not necessarily require union in identity (ittihad fi al-huwiyya), using the example of 'Zayd is blind' (a privative). The commentator critiques a misconception that the author did not claim that lack of distinction entails union in identity but rather union in predication. He then explains the different possible interpretations of the author's intention: either he inferred union of extension from lack of distinction based on absurdities, or he meant union of identity by 'union of extension'. In the first case, lack of distinction of identities does not entail their union; in the second case, the argument is direct but requires that lack of distinction implies identity. The passage ends with a note that the matter requires further consideration (falyata’ammal).

The Existence-Essence Dispute Reduces to Mental Existence
Al-Jurjani reduces the existence-essence dispute to a disagreement over mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the debate over whether existence is additional to essence ultimately depends on whether one affirms mental existence.

Core Idea
The existence-essence dispute hinges on the reality of mental existence.

This passage by al-Jurjani clarifies a key point in the debate between philosophers and theologians. By tracing the arguments of Ibn Sina and al-Shaykh, he shows that the disagreement about whether existence is added to essence in external reality is actually a disagreement about whether existence exists in the mind as a distinct concept. This insight resolves many confusions in later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dense logical flow and how al-Jurjani builds his argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'mental existence' and 'secondary intelligibles'.
2
Identify the two groups
Identify the philosophers who affirm mental existence and those like al-Shaykh who deny it. Note their agreement on external union and disagreement on mental distinction.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's point
Examine the citation from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. Understand why he calls existence a 'secondary intelligible' and what it implies about its reality.
Secondary intelligibles are concepts that do not correspond to external entities but arise from mental operations.
4
See the reduction
Follow al-Jurjani's conclusion: the whole dispute about whether existence is additional reduces to the dispute about mental existence. Consider why this must be so.
Those who deny mental existence say existence = essence externally; those who affirm it say existence is mentally distinct.
5
Note the criticism
Observe al-Jurjani's criticism of later scholars who claim existence is additional while denying mental existence. Reflect on why this is inconsistent.
He also notes that even deniers of mental existence often agree that existence is additional in the mental sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها

نعم لما أثبت الحكماء الوجود الذهني فإنهم وإن وافقوه في ذلك أي وافقوا الشيخ في أن الوجود الخارجي لا يمتاز عن الماهية في الخارج، بل هما متحدان هوية قالوا بأن الوجود يغاير الحقيقة الخارجية ذهنا، فإنه إذا تصور الماهية الموجودة في الخارج فصلها العقل إلى أمرين: ماهية ووجود خارجي، فيحصل هناك صورتان مطابقتان للماهية الخارجية على قياس ما قيل في الجنس والفصل. ثم استشهد على أنهم وافقوا الشيخ في الاتحاد بحسب الخارج وإن خالفوه في التغاير بحسب الذهن بقوله: فصرح ابن سينا في الشفاء أنه من المعقولات الثانية، فليس في الأعيان عين هو وجود أو شيء، إنما الموجود أو الشيء في الخارج جواد أو إنسان أو غيرهما من الحقائق، فهذه الماهيات موجودات عينية متأصلة في الوجود، وأما الوجود والشيئية فلا تأصل لهما في الأعيان، بل هما من المعقولات الثانية التي تعرض للمعقولات الأولى من حيث إنها في الذهن ولا يحاذى بها أمر في الخارج. قال المصنف: فإذن النزاع في أن الوجود زائد أو ليس بزائد راجع إلى النزاع في الوجود الذهني، فمن لم يثبته كالشيخ قال إن الوجود الخارجي عين الماهية مطلقا، ومن أثبته قال الوجود الخارجي زائد على الماهية في الذهن، فمن ادعى من المتأخرين في أن الوجود زائد مع أنه ناف للوجود الذهني لم يكن على بصيرة في دعواه هذه. ولهذا اتفق الجمهور من القائلين بنفي الوجود الذهني على أن الوجود زائد على الماهية ذهاباً إلى المعنى الأول.
The First Observatory - The Third Purpose: That existence is the same as essence or a part of it. Yes, when the philosophers affirmed mental existence, they, although they agreed with him (al-Shaykh) in that external existence does not differ from essence in the external world, rather they are united in identity, they said that existence differs from the external reality mentally, for when the existing essence is conceived in the external world, the mind divides it into two things: essence and external existence, so two forms occur corresponding to the external essence on the analogy of what was said about genus and differentia. Then he cited as evidence that they agreed with al-Shaykh in union externally, though they differed from him in distinction mentally, saying: Ibn Sina stated in al-Shifa that it is among the secondary intelligibles; there is no entity in external reality that is existence or thing; rather the existent or thing externally is a horse, a man, or other realities. These essences are concrete existents rooted in existence, but existence and thingness have no root in external reality; rather they are among the secondary intelligibles that attach to primary intelligibles insofar as they are in the mind, and they correspond to nothing externally. The author said: So the dispute about whether existence is additional or not additional reduces to the dispute about mental existence. Whoever does not affirm it, like al-Shaykh, says that external existence is identical to essence absolutely; whoever affirms it says that external existence is additional to essence in the mind. Whoever among later scholars claims that existence is additional while denying mental existence is not insightful in this claim. For this reason, the majority of those who deny mental existence agree that existence is additional to essence, going by the first meaning.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage defines the position of philosophers who affirm mental existence and shows how their view relates to the dispute about the relationship between existence and essence. The commentator (al-Jurjani) builds the argument step by step. First, he notes that the philosophers who affirm mental existence agree with al-Shaykh (referring to Suhrawardī or possibly al-Rāzī) that in external reality existence and essence are identical in identity. However, they differ from him in the mental realm: the mind abstracts essence and existence as two distinct concepts, so existence is mentally distinct from essence. To support this, the commentator cites Ibn Sina's Shifa, where Ibn Sina states that existence is a secondary intelligible—it has no external referent but is a concept that applies to primary concepts like 'horse' or 'man'. Thus, external realities are essences that are existent; existence itself does not have a separate external reality. The commentator then presents the key conclusion: the dispute about whether existence is additional to essence reduces entirely to the dispute about the reality of mental existence. Those who deny mental existence (like al-Shaykh) hold that external existence is exactly the same as essence. Those who affirm mental existence hold that external existence is additional to essence in the mind. The commentator criticizes later scholars who claim existence is additional but reject mental existence as inconsistent. Finally, he notes that even most of those who deny mental existence still agree that existence is additional, meaning by 'addition' the conceptual distinction in the mind. This passage is both a clarification of positions and a logical reduction of the core disagreement.

المعقولات الثانية
الماهية
الوجود الخارجي
التغاير
الاتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the dispute about whether existence is additional is about external reality.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that the dispute actually concerns mental existence; externally, all parties agree existence and essence are identical.

Checkpoint

A) The dispute about divine attributes
B) The dispute about mental existence
C) The dispute about the eternity of the world
D) The dispute about causality

Key Takeaway
The dispute over whether existence is additional to essence is fundamentally about mental existence.

Intentional attribution and the problem of two existences
The author explains how intentional abstraction avoids the need for two existences in the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how intentional abstraction allows the Necessary Being to be described with absolute existence without implying two distinct existential realities.

Core Idea
Intentional abstraction makes absolute existence a mental attribute, not a real addition.

In this passage, the author tackles a key objection: if the Necessary Being has a specific existence, how can it also be described with absolute existence without having two existences? The answer lies in the concept of intentional attribution (al-ʿurūḍ al-intizāʿī). The author explains that absolute existence is not a real attribute added to the essence but rather an abstract concept derived from the specific existence itself. This lesson unpacks the argument and its implications for divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage together with its literal translation. Get a general sense of the argument before diving into details.
The passage is dense; reading it at least twice is recommended.
2
Identify the core objection
Focus on the objection that if specific existence exists by itself, it would have two existences (its own and absolute). Try to see why this is a problem for the theologian.
The objection is phrased as 'إن كان موجوداً بنفسه يلزم كونه موجوداً بوجودين'.
3
Understand the answer: intentional attribution
The answer says that the description with absolute existence is intentional (intizāʿī), not real. This means the absolute is an attribute derived from the specific existence itself, not an added reality.
The key term is 'الإتصاف بالوجود المطلق إنتزاعي'.
4
Examine the further objection and refutation
A second objection claims that the incidence of absolute on specific must be mental, implying dependence on the intellect. The author refutes this by explaining that intentional abstraction does not require the existence of the intellect; the essence itself is such that the intellect can abstract absolute existence from it.
This shows that the necessity of absolute existence does not depend on the intellect.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها

الإقتضاء، وإنما لم يجب بأن وجوده الخاص يقتضي بذاته اتصافه بالوجود المطلق إشتقاقاً، مع أنه لا ورود حينئذ للإعتراض بسائر الوجودات الخاصة، لئلا يرد الإعتراض بأن الوجود الخاص، إن كان موجوداً بنفسه يلزم كونه موجوداً بوجودين، وإن لم يكن موجوداً بنفسه، بل بالوجود المطلق ففيه إعتراف بزيادة الوجود الذي به موجوديته وكون ماهيته فرداً للوجود لا يضرنا، ويحتاج إلى الجواب بأنه موجود بنفسه والإتصاف بالوجود المطلق إنتزاعي، فلا يلزم كونه موجودا بوجودين، وحينئذ لا بد من القول بأن مبدأ إنتزاعه ليس أمراً وراء ذلك الوجود الخاص من غير ملاحظة أمر آخر معه لئلا يلزم الإعتراف بزيادة الوجود في الواجب بحسب الذات، وإذا كان مبدأ إنتزاعه نفس الوجود الخاص كان المطلق عارضاً له عروض الكلي لفرده، وكان ذلك الوجود الخاص مقتضياً له إقتضاء الجزئي لكليه فلما كان هذا الجواب بالآخرة محتاجاً إلى ذلك الجواب، إختاره وكذا إندفع ما قيل أن عروض المطلق للخاص ليس خارجياً، وإلا لزم كونه قابلاً وفاعلاً، بل ذهني فيلزم أن لا يكون إقتضاؤه المطلق بالإستقلال لإحتياجه إلى العقل وإلى الحصول فيه، فإنه إنما يرد إذا كان العروض حقيقياً، وأما إذا كان إنتزاعياً فاللازم أن تكون ذاته تعالى في الخارج بحيث إذا لاحظه العقل إنتزع منه الوجود المطلق، ولا يتوقف على وجود العقل فضلاً عن الحصول فيه
The First Observatory - The Third Objective: That existence is identical to essence or a part of it. The implication, and it was not necessary that His specific existence necessitates by itself His being described with absolute existence derivationally, although then there is no occurrence of the objection concerning other specific existences, lest the objection occur that the specific existence, if it exists by itself, would entail its being with two existences, and if it does not exist by itself but by absolute existence, then there is an admission of an increase of existence by which its existent-ness and its essence being an individual of existence does not harm us, and it needs the answer that it exists by itself and the description with absolute existence is intentional (intizā‘ī), so it does not entail its being with two existences. And then it is necessary to say that the principle of its abstraction (mabda' intizā‘ihi) is not something beyond that specific existence without observing another thing with it, lest it necessitate the admission of an increase of existence in the Necessary with respect to essence. And if the principle of its abstraction is the specific existence itself, then the absolute is an accident to it like the accident of the universal to its particular, and that specific existence necessitates it like the necessitation of the particular for its universal. So when this answer was ultimately in need of that answer, he chose it. And likewise is refuted what was said that the incidence of the absolute upon the particular is not external, otherwise it would necessitate its being both recipient and agent, but rather mental, so it would necessitate that its necessitation of the absolute is not independent due to its need for the intellect and for obtaining in it. For it only occurs if the incidence is real, but if it is intentional, then the necessary is that His essence, exalted, is in the external such that when the intellect observes it, it abstracts from it absolute existence, and it does not depend on the existence of the intellect, let alone on obtaining in it.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a proof and defense of the claim that the specific existence of the Necessary Being (al-wājib) necessitates absolute existence (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq) through intentional abstraction (intizā‘), avoiding the objection that the Necessary would then have two existences. The author first presents the objection: if the specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ) exists by itself, then it would have two existences (its own and the absolute); if it exists by absolute existence, then it admits an increase. The answer is that the specific existence exists by itself, and its description with absolute existence is intentional, not real. Thus, the principle of abstraction (mabda' al-intizā‘) is nothing other than the specific existence itself, so the absolute is an accident to it like a universal to its particular. This answer also refutes the charge that the incidence (ʿurūḍ) of the absolute upon the particular is either real (leading to agent-recipient duality) or mental (requiring the intellect). The author explains that if the incidence were real, it would entail the thing being both agent and recipient; but if it is intentional, the essence of the Necessary is such that the intellect abstracts absolute existence from it without depending on the intellect's existence. Thus, the necessity of the absolute existence does not require an independent act, avoiding the problem of two existences.

كلي
جزئي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think intentional abstraction means absolute existence is merely mental and thus not real, so the description is not objective.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that the essence itself is external; the abstraction is from that external reality, so absolute existence is not a mental fabrication but a concept derived from real existence. The necessity of absolute existence does not depend on the intellect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does intentional attribution solve the problem of the Necessary Being having two existences?
A) By affirming that absolute existence is a real attribute added to the essence
B) By stating that specific existence necessitates absolute existence independently of the intellect
C) By explaining that the description with absolute existence is intentional, not real, so no extra existence is added
D) By denying that absolute existence is attributed to the Necessary Being at all

Key Takeaway
Intentional abstraction allows the Necessary Being to be described with absolute existence without adding a real attribute, preserving divine simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's existence, do you instinctively treat 'existence' as something added to His essence? How does this passage expose a subtle form of shirk in your own thinking, and what will you do to purify your conception of God?
?
The author insists that intentional abstraction does not depend on the intellect's existence. Yet, in your own reflection on God, are you relying on your mental constructs as if they were the reality? What would it mean for you to trust that God's essence is truly external and independent of your thoughts?
?

This lesson forces you to grapple with a difficult argument—where is the weak point in your own understanding, and what step will you take to strengthen it today?
